'building', 'footprint']], ['The Code for Sustainable Homes provides a comprehensive measure of the \nsustainability of new homes, ensuring that sustainable homes deliver real \nimprovements, in key areas such as carbon dioxide emissions and water use. ', 'This Technical Guide sets out the requirements for the Code and the process for \nachieving a Code assessment. It aims to make gaining a Code assessment as simple, \ntransparent and rigorous as possible. It also aims to inspire confidence in Code \nassessors, home builders, product manufacturers and consumers. ', 'This new full-colour 2010 edition follows a recent wide-ranging Government \nconsultation that sought views on updating and streamlining the Code, to take \naccount of changes to Part L of the Building Regulations, and to resolve some \nproblems that had arisen during its use.', 'This publication, excluding logos, may be reproduced free of charge in any format or medium for research,  \nprivate study or for internal circulation within an organisation. This is subject to it being reproduced accurately  \nand not used in a misleading context. The material must be acknowledged as Crown copyright and the title of  \nthe publication specified.', '\nAcknowledgements \nThe Code for Sustainable Homes Technical Guidance has been drafted by the \nBREEAM Centre at the Building Research Establishment (BRE) under contract to the \nDepartment for Communities and Local Government.', 'Preface\nThe Code for Sustainable Homes (the Code) is an environmental assessment method \nfor rating and certifying the performance of new homes. It is a national standard \nfor use in the design and construction of new homes with a view to encouraging \ncontinuous improvement in sustainable home building. It was launched in December \n2006 with the publication of Code for Sustainable Homes: A step-change in \nsustainable home building practice (Communities and Local Government, 2006) and \nbecame operational in April 2007. Where Building Regulations apply, compliance is \nnecessary at all times.', 'The implementation of the Code is managed by BRE Global (formerly BRE \nCertification Ltd) under contract to Communities and Local Government under \narrangements based on the EcoHomes© operating systems. BRE Global is the main \nlicence holder. Under the terms of its agreement with Communities and Local \nGovernment, BRE Global issues licences to both assessors and other Code service \nproviders. BRE Global provides training, licensing and registration of Code assessors \nto standards ISO 17024 and EN 45011, within a UKAS accredited ‘competent \nperson’s certification scheme’.', 'Each issue is a source of environmental impact which can be assessed against a \nperformance target and awarded one or more credits. Performance targets are more \ndemanding than the minimum standard needed to satisfy Building Regulations or ', 'In addition to meeting mandatory standards, achievement of the requirements in \neach design category scores a number of percentage points. This establishes the \nCode level or rating for the dwelling. The Code certificate illustrates the rating \nachieved with a row of stars. A blue star is awarded for each level achieved. Where \nan assessment has taken place and no rating is achieved, the certificate states that \nzero stars have been awarded.', ' The purpose of this technical guide is to enable Code service providers and \nlicensed assessors to deliver environmental assessments of new dwellings \non the basis of the Code scheme requirements. The guide includes a list \nof issues associated with the building process which are known to impact \non the environment, and for which performance measures to reduce their \nimpacts can be objectively assessed, evaluated and delivered in a practical \nand cost-effective way by the construction industry. Results of the Code \nassessment are recorded on a certificate assigned to the dwelling. The \nprocess of gaining a Code assessment rating and certificate is described \nin Section 2. The guide includes a comprehensive list of definitions and \nreference material for everyone involved in the process. The system of \nevaluating environmental performance in the Code is both transparent and \nopen to scrutiny. A Technical Group consisting of industry representatives, \nsustainability specialists and Government representatives advise on the \ncontent of the guidance and any necessary updates. Significant future \nchanges will be subject to consultation.', ' The Code for Sustainable Homes is an environmental assessment rating \nmethod for new homes which assesses environmental performance in a \ntwo-stage process (design stage and post construction stage) using objective \ncriteria and verification. The results of the Code assessment are recorded on \na certificate assigned to the dwelling. ', ' The report Code for Sustainable Homes: A step-change in sustainable home \nbuilding practice (Communities and Local Government, 2006) defined a set \nof nine categories of environmental impact, see Table 1.1.', ' The Code assigns one or more performance requirements (assessment \ncriteria) to all of the environmental issues. When each performance \nrequirement is achieved, a credit is awarded (except the three mandatory \nrequirements with no associated credits). The total number of credits \navailable to a category is the sum of credits available for all the issues  \nwithin it.', ' Mandatory minimum performance standards are set for some issues. For \nthree of these, a single mandatory requirement is set which must be met, \nwhatever Code level rating is sought. Credits are not awarded for these \nissues. Confirmation that the performance requirements are met for all three \nis a minimum entry requirement for achieving a Level 1 rating. The three un-\ncredited issues are:', ' If the mandatory minimum performance standard is met for the three  \nun-credited issues, four further mandatory issues need to be considered. \nThese are agreed to be such important issues that separate Government \npolicies are being pursued to mitigate their effects. For two of these, credits \nare awarded for increasing levels of achievement recognised within the \nCode. ', ' The final two issues with mandatory requirements are Fabric Energy \nEfficiency and Lifetime Homes. To achieve an overall Code rating of level 5 it \nis necessary to achieve at lease 7 credits in Ene 2. To achieve an overall Code \nrating of level 6 it is necessary to achieve at least 7 credits in Ene 2 and 3 \ncredits in Hea 4 – Lifetime Homes.', ' Tables 1.2 and 1.3 below illustrate how, for the creditable mandatory issues, \nthe minimum mandatory standards increase with increasing rating levels. For \nCO2 emissions there are increased mandatory minimum standards for each \nincrease in Code level. ', '\n Further credits are available on a free-choice or tradable basis from other \nissues so that the developer may choose how to add performance credits \n(converted through weighting to percentage points) to achieve the rating for \nwhich they are aiming.', ' The environmental impact categories within the Code are not of equal \nimportance. Their relative value is conveyed by applying a consensus-based \nenvironmental weighting factor (see details below) to the sum of all the \nraw credit scores in a category, resulting in a score expressed as percentage \npoints. The points for each category add up to 100, see table 1.4. ', ' Table 1.4 shows how weightings are applied across all Code categories of \nenvironmental impact to adjust the relative values of credits within different \ncategories. Within each category, credits are awarded for achieving specified \ndegrees of performance. The weighting factors show the contribution made \nby each category to the total performance recognised and rewarded by the \nCode. The total available contribution is expressed as 100 per cent. The \nweighting of each category is expressed as a fraction of this, such that the \nsum of all the category contributions equals 100 per cent. ', ' As an example, the 31 credits available for energy and CO2 emissions \ncontribute 36.4 per cent of the total available performance. Similarly, the \nfour credits available for pollution contribute 2.8 per cent of the total \navailable performance. By dividing the weighting factor by the number of \ncredits for each category, we arrive at an approximate weighted value for \neach credit. For instance, within the energy and CO2 emissions category, \n36.4 per cent contribution to the total, divided by the 31 credits available, \nmeans that each credit in this category is worth roughly 1.17 percentage \npoints. Similarly, for the Pollution category, 2.8 per cent contribution to the \ntotal, divided by the four credits available, means that each credit in this \ncategory is worth roughly 0.70 percentage points.', ' It is important to note that weightings apply at the category level and not to \nindividual credits to avoid rounding errors. For instance, for the Energy and \nCO2 Emissions category, 36.4 per cent weighted contribution divided by 31 \ncredits equals 1.1741935 expressed to seven decimal places, but 1.17 when \nexpressed to two decimal places.', ' It is also important to note that achieving high performance in one \ncategory of environmental impact can sometimes result in a lower level of \nperformance for another. For instance, if biomass is used to meet heating \ndemand, credits will be available for performance in respect of energy \nsupplied from a renewable source, but credits cannot be awarded for low \nNOX emissions. It is therefore impossible to achieve a total percentage points \nscore of 100. ', '• The mandatory requirements for CO2 emissions, fabric energy efficiency, \ninternal water use and Lifetime homes should be checked and confirmed \nat the minimum values required to meet the Code level sought.', ' If any of the standards for the three non-creditable issues are not met, then a \nzero rating will result, regardless of the other credits achieved, including the \ncreditable mandatory issues.', ' If all the non-creditable mandatory standards are met, but one or other \nof the creditable mandatory issues fails to reach the minimum required to \nachieve a higher level, the rating will be determined by the lowest mandatory \nlevel met. ', ' Figure 1.3 illustrates the calculation method to obtain the total percentage \npoints score. For each category, the number of credits achieved is divided by \nthe total available and multiplied by the category weighting factor to derive \nthe percentage points score. This number should be rounded down to \ntwo decimal places before the next step.', ' The rounded percentage points scores for each category are then summed \nto arrive at the total percentage points score for the dwelling. The total \npercentage points score must be rounded down to the nearest whole \nnumber.', ' The Code level is then derived from the total percentage points according to \ntable 1.6. Each Code level is represented on the certificate by an equivalent \nnumber of stars from 1 to 6.', '2. The appointed Code assessor registers the development formally with \nthe service provider as early as possible. This registration is valid for a \nmaximum of five years between expiry of the Technical Guide version at \nregistration and submission of the design stage report. So far, there is no \ntime limit on the delivery of the associated post construction stage or final \ncertificates for the development. This enables the version of the Code to \nbe set such that even if the Code is updated the version used can remain \nthe same across the site. ', '3. It is only possible to register developments/dwellings against the current \nversion of the Code for Sustainable Homes. The only exception is where \nthere is a documented contractual or legal requirement to meet a specific \nlevel of the Code against a previous version. Documentary evidence \nwould need to be provided to the Code service provider in order to have \nthis exception approved prior to assessment taking place. Sites already \nregistered under a previous version can be re-registered under the current \nversion of the Code. ', ' The assessment process for these two stages is very similar. Evidence is \ncollated and used as the basis for the assessor to determine how many \ncredits are to be awarded for each issue. A summary report is submitted to \nthe Code service provider for quality assurance and certification.', ' The licensed organisation has responsibility for the assessment and the Code \nassessor has responsibility for producing the report. It is possible for different \nlicensed organisations to undertake the DS and PCS assessments for a \nparticular site. Code assessors are required to make DS assessment evidence \navailable to the post construction assessor where this is the case.', ' Licensed organisations are required to keep full supporting documentation \nof all assessments carried out. Assessors are required to retain this \ninformation for a period of 10 years from submission of the report. Each \nlicensed organisation is required to establish and maintain internal quality \nmanagement procedures relating to record-keeping. ', ' The DS assessment is carried out on the detailed design of each dwelling in \nthe period up to the issue of tender documents, sometimes referred to as \nRIBA Stages A–G. A Code assessment requires a more detailed specification \nthan Building Regulations compliance because the Code covers many more \naspects of the performance of the dwelling. For instance, specification details \nof all the sanitary fittings are required to calculate the score for the Code \nindoor water issue.', ' The assessor will also be able to calculate, at this initial design stage \nassessment, any site-related credits. Therefore, details of final landscaping \nand ecological enhancement measures will also need to be known.', '• Demonstrate that the performance requirements set for each of the three \nnon-creditable mandatory issues of environmental impact are met for each \ndwelling.', '• Demonstrate that the performance requirements set for each of the \ncredited mandatory issues of environmental impact (dwelling emission \nrate, fabric energy efficiency, indoor water use and Lifetime Homes) are \nmet for the desired rating for each dwelling.', '• Evaluate the performance of each dwelling against the requirements set \nfor each of the chosen issues to confirm that the required standards are \nmet for the desired overall rating.', ' When the assessor is satisfied with the performance under the Code, \nthey shall submit the assessment report to the service provider for quality \nassurance and to receive design stage or interim Code certification. The \nreport must contain references to documentary evidence describing how \nperformance requirements are met, creating a clear audit trail linking the \nrating awarded to the evidence used to justify it. The service provider may \nissue a standard report format for the assessor to complete. ', ' If a DS assessment has been undertaken, this can be used as the starting \npoint for the PCS assessment. The PCS assessment is carried out to confirm \nthat dwellings are either built to the DS specifications or, if there are \nvariations from the DS, that they are documented, reassessed, and a new \nscore and Code level calculated for each dwelling that is affected. The \nassessor organisation should carry out an appropriate level of checking to be \nsatisfied that commitments made have been achieved. ', '• An appropriate number of site visits for each development site (at least \nonce per site). The appropriate number of site visits is dependent on the \nparticular situation. It is the assessor’s responsibility to determine the \nnecessary number of site visits required to satisfy themselves that the \nassessment properly reflects the dwelling', '• Every different specification for each issue shall be reviewed and evidence \nrecorded to confirm that it complies with the requirements for PCS \nassessment', '• Where dwellings of identical construction type are planned to be \nreleased in phases, the assessment evidence must show that the same \nspecifications have been incorporated into dwellings completed following \nthe initial release. If there are variances to the specification in subsequent \nreleases, the assessor shall reassess the dwellings based on the new \nspecifications.', ' The number of PCS assessment reports is likely to be based on the phased \ncompletion of dwellings within a development. The number of PCS \nassessment reports submitted to cover a particular development is entirely at \nthe discretion of the assessor (and developer).', ' For a large or phased development, there are some issues that will not be \ncomplete when a post construction stage assessment for the first dwelling(s) \nis submitted to the service provider. For example, these could include some \necology credits or community-based facilities. It is possible to certify the \nfirst dwellings without certain issues being complete. The details of these \nexceptions are contained within the relevant issues, but are also highlighted \nin table 2.1 below. This is not intended to be a comprehensive list; its \npurpose is to provide guidance to assessors on the type of exceptions that \nare acceptable. ', 'Centralised energy supply infrastructure on multi-phase \ndevelopments may not be commissioned in the first phase but \nshould be operational before more than 60 per cent of the \ndwellings are completed/certified. This requirement is variable \nwhere there is an alternative statutory requirement in place for \nthe system to be operational at a different stage.', 'For community heating systems; the infrastructure to allow a \nfuture connection must be provided to each dwelling for credits \nto be awarded, regardless of the percentage of total dwellings \ncompleted.', 'Provision of external drying fittings or footings for later parts of \nthe development, as this would form part of the ‘externals’ and \nbe one of the last finishes.', 'Centralised energy supply infrastructure on multi-phase \ndevelopments may not be commissioned in the first phase but \nshould be operational before more than 60 per cent of the \ndwellings are completed/certified. This requirement is variable \nwhere there is an alternative statutory requirement in place for \nthe system to be operational at a different stage.', 'For community heating systems; the infrastructure to allow a \nfuture connection must be provided to each dwelling for credits \nto be awarded, regardless of the percentage of total dwellings \ncompleted.', 'Ene 8: Cycle storage Some cycle storage may not be in place in later stages of the \ndevelopment as this would form part of the ‘externals’ and be \nthe last of the finishes installed.', 'Communal grey water or rainwater systems may not be installed \nyet but should be operational before more than 60 per cent of \nthe dwellings are completed/certified. ', 'Communal rainwater collection systems may not be installed \nyet but should be operational before more than 60 per cent \nof the dwellings are completed/certified. (Water butts should \nbe installed although they are often left until just before \ncompletion.)', 'Specific infiltration measures, such as soakaways and permeable \npaving, may not be fully installed when initial phases are \nreleased, but evidence of the devices to be used and any relevant \ncalculations need to be available. They need to be operational \nbefore more than 60 per cent of the dwellings are completed/\ncertified.', 'All of the flood protection measures should be planned and a \nprogramme for their construction finalised which indicates that \nthey will be operational before more than 60 per cent of the \ndwellings are completed/certified.', 'Local authority collection schemes, kitchen waste collection \nschemes and private recycling schemes will need to be \noperational within one year of the completion date of this \ndwelling.', 'Centralised energy supply infrastructure on multi-phase \ndevelopments may not be commissioned in the first phase but \nshould be operational before more than 60 per cent of the \ndwellings are completed/certified. This requirement is variable \nwhere there is an alternative statutory requirement in place for \nthe system to be operational at a different stage.', 'For community heating systems; the infrastructure to allow a \nfuture connection must be provided to each dwelling for credits \nto be awarded, regardless of the percentage of total dwellings \ncompleted.', 'It will not be possible to check that all features outside of the \nconstruction zone have been undisturbed until construction is \ncompleted.', 'Features are often not in place until the whole site is complete or \nuntil the appropriate planting season. A design and programme \nare required; demonstrating that all such enhancement works \nwill be completed normally within a single annual cycle from the \ncompletion of construction works on the site’s final dwellings.', 'It will not be possible to confirm that the credit has been \nachieved for an individual plot being assessed until the entire \ndevelopment site has been assessed to confirm all features have \nbeen protected. For areas where construction is not yet \ncomplete, it should be checked that protection measures are in \nplace to protect features during construction works as detailed at \ndesign stage.', 'Planting is often delayed until the whole site is complete. A \ndesign and programme are required; demonstrating that all such \nenhancement works will be completed normally within a single \nannual cycle from the completion of construction works on the \nsite’s final dwellings.', ' The information within this section will clarify how the evidence requirements \ntables should be used, the roles and responsibilities of individuals involved \nand the definitions of the terminology used within the tables. Clarification \nof the routes to certification has also been provided to emphasise the \nimportance of the design stage assessment.', ' The Schedule of Evidence Required lists the basic information that is needed \nto demonstrate compliance for certification. They are not exhaustive lists \nof every document necessary to conduct the assessment or calculate \nperformance. ', ' The role of the Code assessor is to undertake credit score calculations and \nverify the validity and authenticity of evidence provided. The assessor will \nalso carry out calculations which are pertinent only to the Code assessment, \nwhere these are not undertaken by a professional under an appropriate \nregulatory requirement, competent persons scheme or specialist requirement. \nAt the post construction stage, the assessor is required to carry out sufficient \nsite inspections to audit the as-built evidence. At least one visit will be \nrequired for all sites. Each specification employed to meet the criteria at the \ndesign stage and/or post construction stage must be reviewed or assessed as \nappropriate, and detailed documentary evidence recorded, to confirm that it \ncomplies with the requirements for post construction assessment given in the \nTechnical Guidance manual. The Code assessor is at all times responsible for \nthe content and scoring within the assessment report.', ' It may be that assessors have competencies in other disciplines and as \nsuch are employed to undertake additional tasks that feed into the Code \nassessment, but these fall outside their role as an assessor. ', ' The Code service provider operates the UKAS Accredited Product Scheme for \nthe Code to ensure that assessors are technically competent, accurate and \nprofessional when carrying out assessment services on behalf of their clients. \nThe service provider will audit assessment services for quality assurance (QA) \npurposes. The QA process is in place to help protect the credibility of both ', 'the Code scheme and the assessor, as well as to safeguard the interests of \ndevelopers and their customers. Through a process of random checking, the \nQA audit will provide a reasonable degree of confidence that assessment \nreports correctly reflect the requirements of the scheme, so allowing a \ncertificate to be issued. The QA process will not seek to check every aspect of \na Code assessment. ', ' The developer is responsible for providing accurate evidence as required by \nthe assessor to carry out their assessment. At post construction stage, the \ndeveloper must confirm which dwellings are built to the same specification as \nthose types inspected by the assessor on site. ', ' At post construction stage, written confirmation from the developer must \nbe in the form of a letter on company headed paper and must be signed by \na senior individual who is deemed responsible for the development by the \nboard of directors or equivalent senior management group of that developer. \nThe letter must refer to specific criteria within the issues and explain how the \ncriteria or design stage commitment have been satisfied. The letter must be \nexplicit in terms of the assessment and the site under development, and must \nconfirm which dwellings are built to the same specification as those types \ninspected by the assessor on site.', ' ‘Detailed documentary evidence’ may be any written documentation \nconfirming compliance. Across the assessment, evidence will include a mix \nof letters, the site inspection report, and specification text or drawings as \nappropriate. The assessor must satisfy themselves that the evidence is robust \nand traceable. A letter of intent from the developer is not acceptable where \nthe requirement is for ‘detailed documentary evidence’.', ' Assessors may ask others to complete the site inspection on their behalf, \nin which case the assessor will be responsible for ensuring that their \nrepresentative is competent and able to carry out the task.', 'that must be provided in the report. The report must include sufficient \ninformation to create an audit trail that justifies the conclusions reached, \nparticularly where the site report is the only evidence supplied at post \nconstruction stage. A simple statement confirming compliance will not \nbe sufficient. Photographs may be useful as supporting evidence in a \nreport, though it is unlikely that a photograph on its own will demonstrate \ncompliance. The report template allows the assessor to reference \nphotographs should they choose to provide them.', ' As many issues as possible should be checked during the site inspection. \nHowever, it is unlikely that all issues will be checked during one site visit. \nDifferent specifications of the dwelling will be completed at different times, \nso the site inspection will need to be planned during discussion with the \nclient to identify the most appropriate time. At least one site visit must be \ncarried out for every assessment.', ' At post construction stage, written confirmation from the developer must \nbe in the form of a letter on company headed paper and must be signed by \na senior individual who is deemed responsible for the development by the \nboard of directors or equivalent senior management group of that developer. \nThe letter must refer to specific criteria within the issues and explain how the \ncriteria or design stage commitment have been satisfied. The letter must be \nexplicit in terms of the assessment and the site under development. ', '1. Complete both the design stage (DS) assessment and the post \nconstruction stage (PCS) assessment. 'The Code for Sustainable Homes provides a comprehensive measure of the \nsustainability of new homes, ensuring that sustainable homes deliver real \nimprovements, in key areas such as carbon dioxide emissions and water use. ', 'This Technical Guide sets out the requirements for the Code and the process for \nachieving a Code assessment. It aims to make gaining a Code assessment as simple, \ntransparent and rigorous as possible. It also aims to inspire confidence in Code \nassessors, home builders, product manufacturers and consumers.
